  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer6553696410128182609">The year is 1952, and E.F. Bloodworth has returned to his home - a forgotten corner of Tennessee - after twenty years of roaming. The wife he walked out on has withered and faded. His three sons are grown and angry. Warren is a womanising alcoholic; Boyd is driven by jealousy to hunt down his wife's lover; and Brady puts hexes on his enemies from his mother's porch. Only F</span>
  <span id="freeText6553696410128182609" style="display:none">The year is 1952, and E.F. Bloodworth has returned to his home - a forgotten corner of Tennessee - after twenty years of roaming. The wife he walked out on has withered and faded. His three sons are grown and angry. Warren is a womanising alcoholic; Boyd is driven by jealousy to hunt down his wife's lover; and Brady puts hexes on his enemies from his mother's porch. Only Fleming, the old man's grandson, treats him with respect and sees past all the hatred, realising the way it can poison a man's soul. It is ultimately the love of Raven Lee, a sloe-eyed beauty from another town, that gives Fleming the courage to reject his family's curse.<br /><br />In a tale redolent with the crumbling loyalties and age-old strife of the post-war American South made familiar to us by Cormac McCarthy, Gay's characters inhabit a world driven by blood ties that strangle as they bind. A coming of age novel, a love story, and a portrait of a family torn apart, <b>Provinces of Night</b> introduced a distinctive new voice in American fiction and a superb cast of characters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6553696410128182609" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });
      $('rating_details_tip').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });

<div id="review_2503702559" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2503702559" />
    <a title="Candi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3639005-candi"><img alt="Candi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585187464p2/3639005.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2503702559?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Candi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Candi" href="/user/show/3639005-candi">Candi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385499286" href="/book/show/1608946.Provinces_of_Night">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3639005-candi?shelf=southern-gothic">southern-gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3639005-candi?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3639005-candi?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3639005-candi?shelf=anneautumn2018challenge">anneautumn2018challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2503702559" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8653461750613731068">I’m not sure how a book about such down and out characters – some of them violent, others bitter and unhinged, some vengeful, and others simply lost and lonely – could contain such beauty. And yet, it does. This southern gothic tale is refined and exquisitely written. Honestly, it was such a delightful surprise to discover the talents of William Gay, an author I had never read before this. <br/> <br/>This is a story of the Bloodworth family. The name is fitting indeed. The old man, E.F., and his three son</span>
  <span id="freeText8653461750613731068" style="display:none">I’m not sure how a book about such down and out characters – some of them violent, others bitter and unhinged, some vengeful, and others simply lost and lonely – could contain such beauty. And yet, it does. This southern gothic tale is refined and exquisitely written. Honestly, it was such a delightful surprise to discover the talents of William Gay, an author I had never read before this. <br /> <br />This is a story of the Bloodworth family. The name is fitting indeed. The old man, E.F., and his three sons, Warren, Boyd and Brady, and his grandson, Fleming are the men of the family. When E.F. took off decades ago, he left behind a wife and three sons without benefit of a father figure. Not that E.F. was much of a father – no, he was a whiskey-making, pistol-toting, formidable man that enjoyed picking his banjo and doing whatever it was that satisfied E.F. He was a legend in Ackerman’s Field long before he was sent off to the state penitentiary. His stories are still told on front porches across town. Now the old man feels his time is coming to an end and he wants to make amends with the family he deserted so long ago.<br /><br /><i>"These hours before first light were merciless. You could not go back to sleep and it was too early to get up and the things you had done or not done lay in your mind immovable as misshapen things you’d erected from stone. There was no give to these hours. They took no prisoners, made no compromises, and the things you had done could not be rationalized into anything save things you had done. The past was bitter and dry and ashes in his mouth, its bone arms clasped him like some old desiccated lover he could not be shut of."</i><br /><br />E.F. Bloodworth has packed up his guitar and his banjo and is headed back to Ackerman’s Field, where he will meet his grandson, Fleming, for the first time. Much like his father and uncles before him, Fleming knows all about abandonment. His mother fled town with a peddler and his father is dead-set on catching up with them and seeking his revenge. Now Fleming is alone in the little ramshackle house -alone with his books and his dreams of writing. At seventeen years old, he is sharp and philosophical. A kid I wished I could squeeze tight and tell him everything would be all right! He has a friend named Junior Albright, a guy that through a series of bumbles added much needed levity to a book that could otherwise have been very dark. Well, it is dark, but here and there are these bursts of hilarity that nearly had me in stitches! All of the characters are so deftly drawn and I was completely taken in by the whole bunch. The beautiful Raven Lee, the muddled yet kind-hearted Uncle Warren, and even the unbalanced and hostile, card-reading, hex-cursing, Uncle Brady. <br /><br />I love music; I guess you could say we are a musical family – although not one of us earns a living this way. It’s something that we simply have to have in our lives to make us happy – the kiddos included. This book has a thread of music running throughout, and I loved this aspect - imagining these old-timers composing their songs, sitting in their rockers while crowds gathered round to listen. <br /><br /><i>"The music told itself, it made some obscure connection for which there were no words. The music was its own story, but a man could dip into the vast reservoir of folk and blues lines and phrases and images and construct his own story: though upon performing it and without it losing any relevance to his own life it now belonged to the audience as well… The old songs with juryrigged verses like bodies cobbled up out of bones from a thousand skeletons. Songs about death and lost love and rambling down the line because sometimes down the line was the only place left. Songs that treated the most desperate of loss with a dark sardonic humor… There was an eerie timelessness about it that said it could have been written a thousand years ago, or it could have been an unfinished song about events that had not yet played themselves out."</i><br /><br />I suspect my review is meandering a bit. Plot-wise, the story ambles along at a leisurely pace. I say this in a positive way, as I found so much more to absorb than just the plot. It is a character-driven story that excels in highlighting the reality of the lives of these people. I could picture each and every one of them so vividly. I could hear the rhythm of their voices. Feel the pain of their sorrows. The tension heightens by book’s end and I was left with my own sadness that it was finished. I wasn’t quite ready to leave some of these characters just yet. A new book and author to grace my favorites shelf, <b>Provinces of Night</b> is one I highly recommend!<br /><br /><i>"Life blindsides you so hard you can taste the bright copper blood in your mouth then it beguiles you with a gift of profound and appalling beauty."</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="8653461750613731068" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

            Shelves:

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51089-jamie?shelf=crimes-and-capers">crimes-and-capers</a>,

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer203682521" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4312660609416938288">I’m going to go with the best way I know to put this. If you took what I love about Cormac McCarthy, and what I love about <i>Justified</i>, and what I love about home, the center of that Venn diagram is this book.<br/><br/>Meaning, there wasn’t a pleasure center in my brain this didn’t light up like Cleveland. Meaning, if there were any folks I’d bring to life out of a book it’s the Bloodworths out of this one.<br/><br/>* * *<br/><br/><strong>January 2013:</strong> If this was my favorite book of 2011, and my favorite book of 2012, kicking the yea</span>
  <span id="freeText4312660609416938288" style="display:none">I’m going to go with the best way I know to put this. If you took what I love about Cormac McCarthy, and what I love about <i>Justified</i>, and what I love about home, the center of that Venn diagram is this book.<br /><br />Meaning, there wasn’t a pleasure center in my brain this didn’t light up like Cleveland. Meaning, if there were any folks I’d bring to life out of a book it’s the Bloodworths out of this one.<br /><br />* * *<br /><br /><strong>January 2013:</strong> If this was my favorite book of 2011, and my favorite book of 2012, kicking the year off with it means it can be my favorite book of 2013 too, right? Just kidding. Trick question. There is no question.<br /><br />I read this nine times this past year. Nine. I don’t know when I’ve had more of a favorite book. It puts its fingers on the keys and nails every chord. </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1010241859" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1010241859" />
    <a title="Howard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32041201-howard"><img alt="Howard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587918150p2/32041201.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1010241859?book_show_action=true">Oct 15, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Howard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Howard" href="/user/show/32041201-howard">Howard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=american-south">american-south</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=brothers">brothers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=family">family</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=fathers-and-sons">fathers-and-sons</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=mothers-and-sons">mothers-and-sons</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=family-dysfunction">family-dysfunction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=favorite">favorite</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1010241859" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14696853551210521629"><b>“I never wanted a lot of money out of it, or to be a literary lion. I just wanted to be a writer.” -- William Gay</b><br/><br/><br/>I liked this novel by the late William Gay. I really liked it. It made me wonder why I had waited so long to read something by him. It isn’t like I had never heard of him. A friend recommended his short stories to me long before I joined Goodreads.<br/><br/>I liked it so well that by the time I was about half-way through <i>Provinces of Night,</i> which is his second novel, I ordered his first, <i>The Lo</i></span>
  <span id="freeText14696853551210521629" style="display:none"><b>“I never wanted a lot of money out of it, or to be a literary lion. I just wanted to be a writer.” -- William Gay</b><br /><br /><br />I liked this novel by the late William Gay. I really liked it. It made me wonder why I had waited so long to read something by him. It isn’t like I had never heard of him. A friend recommended his short stories to me long before I joined Goodreads.<br /><br />I liked it so well that by the time I was about half-way through <i>Provinces of Night,</i> which is his second novel, I ordered his first, <i>The Long Home.</i><br /><br />As a person, William Gay seemed to be unpretentious and down-to-earth, and he “just wanted to be a writer.” That urge was kindled when he was a teenager, sparked by a teacher who loaned him a copy of Thomas Wolfe’s novel, <i>Look Homeward, Angel.</i> He said that it was that book that exerted the greatest impact on his desire to become a writer. <br /><br />That incident found its way into <i>Provinces of Night:</i><br /> <br /><b>“What did you think of that book that I gave you?<br />It’s the best book that I ever read.<br />There’s another book, a sort of sequel to it called <i>Of Time and the River</i>. It continues the story of Eugene Gant. There’s a very powerful scene where old man Gant dies. Would you like me to bring it to you?<br />Well, I’d like to read it. I could pick it up somewhere.<br />No, I’d like to bring it. I have a lot of books and books are better if you can share them.”</b> <br /><br /><br />Gay’s first submitted manuscript was written in long hand. On the rejection slip that he received was a note that all submissions must be typewritten. He didn’t have a typewriter. <br /><br />He also included that incident in his novel:<br /><br /><b>“Crossing the ditch before the hill began its steep ascent [Fleming] opened the flap of the envelope. The first thing he saw was his own handwriting, the second a note that had been paperclipped to his manuscript. We regret that we are unable to read handwritten manuscripts, someone in Atlanta had written. All submissions must be typewritten. He threw the manuscript into the ditch and went on up the hill but after a few steps returned and recovered the manila envelope and went on.”</b> <br /><br /><br />William Elbert Gay (1941-2012), the son of a sharecropper and sawmill worker, was born in Hohenwald, Tennessee. After serving in the U.S. Navy during the Vietnam War, he lived in New York and Chicago. In 1978, however, he returned to the area where he was born and lived there until his death.<br /><br />For years he worked as a carpenter, drywall hanger, and painter, all the while composing stories in his head, stories that he wrote down on paper after work. The stories contained many episodes that were based on his life and experiences. “He just wanted to be a writer,” and he became a published one when two of his short stories were published in a literary magazine in 1998. He was fifty-seven years old. <br /><br />In 1999, he published his first novel, <i>The Long Home,</i> which received good reviews and led to comparisons to Larry Brown and Barry Hannah. One critic wrote that the book didn’t feel like a debut novel. Perhaps that was because he had been practicing for forty years.<br /><br /><br /><b>“There was something oddly restful about the fireflies. He couldn’t put his finger on it but he drew comfort from it anyway. The way they’d seemed not separate entities but a single being, a moving river of light that flowed above the dark water like its negative image and attained a transient and fragile dimension over the provinces of night.”</b><br /><br /><br /><i>Provinces of Night</i> is set in 1952 in the Tennessee hills southeast of Nashville. It is where William Gay was born and the place he returned to in 1978. It is the story of three generations of males who were born and raised in that same area.<br /><br />The two older generations are nonconformists who struggle to deal with forces that lead them astray and tear at the fabric of weakening family ties that are incapable of holding them. It began with a father who abandoned his wife and three sons twenty years ago and has now returned home in an effort to make amends, if it is not too late. <br /><br />One son has affection for his father, but is struggling with his own life, made all the more difficult by his alcoholism. Another son is consumed by a desire for revenge, the need to track down his wife who deserted him and his son by running off with another man, and who isn’t concerned one way or the other with the return of his father. The youngest son, for reasons that become apparent, was affected the most by his father’s disappearance and then by his reappearance. He is the one who never married, the one who remained behind to care for his mother. But the experience has unmoored him to the degree that he places hexes on all who disappoint him. And many people disappoint him.<br /><br />Then there is the youngest generation, represented by seventeen-year old Fleming, who, since his mother’s desertion and his father’s far-ranging search for her, lives alone. It is his coming of age that is the heart and soul of the story. Although it is never explicitly stated, he is the last chance for the family to redeem itself. <br /><br />If one reads the publisher’s synopsis, or what I just wrote, one would think that this book is a real downer. Yes, there is darkness, but there is also much humor, especially when one Junior Albright makes his appearances. Without Junior, the story would be one of almost unalleviated sadness and despair. He might not be all bright, but he does display a degree of shrewdness when he finds himself in a tight spot, which is where he often finds himself.<br /><br />The plot builds leisurely as the principal characters are established, but that's okay. The passages that describe the place and the people are lyrical and made me want to slow down and read them again.<br /> <br /><br /><b>“It was on a Saturday morning and there was a lot of folks stirring about. People haulin’ cotton into town on wagons to the gin. Folks come in on Saturday to trade. Saturday was a big day back then. Saturday was what got you through the week.”<br /><br /><br />“From the shade of the ivy-covered end of Itchy Mama Baker’s porch the old men in ladderback chairs and tilting Coke crates watched the hot blacktop that snaked up the grade toward Ackerman’s Field three miles away. They’d sit daylong and wait for something to happen, anything to happen, waiting for the road to entertain them.”<br /><br /><br />“What he’d seen of life had shown him that the world had little of comfort or assurance. He suspected that there were no givens, no map through the maze…. Life blindsides you so hard you can taste the bright copper blood in your mouth then it beguiles you with a gift of profound and appalling beauty.”</b><br /><br /><br />But once the characters are established the story picks up the pace and expertly alternates between dark despair and lighthearted humor. That is a mix that has always appealed to me, I suppose, because it aptly describes the lives of so many people.<br /><br />Did I mention that I liked this book – really liked it -- so much so that it now has a place on my "favorites" shelf? It might have been my first William Gay novel, but it will not be the last. <i>The Long Home</i> is waiting on my shelf, but I don’t plan to keep it waiting much longer.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14696853551210521629" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2064728270264529466">Clifton, Tennessee and thereabouts.  The year is 1952 and the story centers on E.F. Bloodworth, his progeny, and all their doin's.  Within these pages you will find a houseful of clocks, but where time no longer matters.  There is a woman who is stingy with her words, and the sound of a river as it talks to itself.  Gathering places include a beer joint called Goblin's Knob serving Falstaff beer, and Itchy Mama's, where, if you dare, you can join in a "vicious cutthroat" game of Rook.  A man set</span>
  <span id="freeText2064728270264529466" style="display:none">Clifton, Tennessee and thereabouts.  The year is 1952 and the story centers on E.F. Bloodworth, his progeny, and all their doin's.  Within these pages you will find a houseful of clocks, but where time no longer matters.  There is a woman who is stingy with her words, and the sound of a river as it talks to itself.  Gathering places include a beer joint called Goblin's Knob serving Falstaff beer, and Itchy Mama's, where, if you dare, you can join in a "vicious cutthroat" game of Rook.  A man sets himself to rewriting the Bible by omitting all the "begats" and "wherefores" so as to make it more understandable to regular folk. <br />   <br />I closed the cover of this book with a deep feeling of melancholy.  It is well nigh impossible not to compare the writing to that of Cormac McCarthy, although I did not find this quite as dark.  Boy howdy, what a storyteller! </span>

<div id="review_2504471875" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2504471875" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5512657-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543780395p2/5512657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2504471875?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/5512657-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385499279" href="/book/show/953378.Provinces_of_Night">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=2018-aty-challenge">2018-aty-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=southern-lit">southern-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=kirk-s-list">kirk-s-list</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2504471875" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8245664887619886757">It always makes my soul sing a bit to find an author who really understands the South, someone who can look at the depressing and seedy side and not make it seem composed of only depravity. Oh, William Gay finds the depravity, but he also sees the warmth, the desperation and the humor. I laughed hard, cringed a bit, and felt an emotional lump in my throat.<br/><br/>The Bloodworths of Tennessee are a hard-scrabble bunch. At least one of them is certifiably nuts, some drink too much, guns are a common part </span>
  <span id="freeText8245664887619886757" style="display:none">It always makes my soul sing a bit to find an author who really understands the South, someone who can look at the depressing and seedy side and not make it seem composed of only depravity. Oh, William Gay finds the depravity, but he also sees the warmth, the desperation and the humor. I laughed hard, cringed a bit, and felt an emotional lump in my throat.<br /><br />The Bloodworths of Tennessee are a hard-scrabble bunch. At least one of them is certifiably nuts, some drink too much, guns are a common part of their life, and the threads that bind them together are brittle. The patriarch, E.F., left years before, taking with him only a banjo and his music, but he is coming home, old and sick, and not everyone, particularly his son, Brady, is happy to see him again. In such a family, it is hard to know where loyalty should lie and what love truly is, and seventeen year-old Fleming has been dropped into this crazy bunch and has to try to find his feet and his life.<br /><br />The story is great. I kept wishing life would leave me alone and let me read without interruption. But, beyond that, the skill of the writer is amazing.<br /><br /><i>He began to suspect another, deeper layer of time, a time of stone and cloud and tree to which the time of clocks and calendars was a gross mockery cobbled up by savages. He felt the ways of men fall from him like sundered shackles.</i> <br /><br />He commands his environment and his characters, and he causes you to feel the pain and angst and loneliness.<br /><br /><i>Before she shared his bed, life had been pointless, but now it had become unbearable. She had appeared from nowhere and returned to it, but she’d taken over his life, left with a lien on his body, a mortgage on his soul.</i><br /><br />The passage from which he takes the novel’s title read more like poetry than prose:<br /><br /><i>There was something oddly restful about the fireflies. He couldn’t put his finger on it but he drew comfort from it anyway. The way they’d seemed not separate entities but a single being, a moving river of light that flowed above the dark water like its negative image and attained a transient and fragile dominion over the provinces of night.</i><br /><br />I could quote dozens of passage that rise off the page and stand before you with body, corporeal somehow, vividly real.<br /><br /> <i>There was a ceiling fan turning above him and he lay watching light play on the revolving blade. His life had honed itself down to a finite number of revelations of a metal blade through dead air.</i><br /><br />I guess it is evident that I found this book magical, riveting, starkly real. My thanks once again to The Southern Literary Trail for introducing me to another superb writer that I would have completely missed otherwise. <br /><br />Finally, Why was I not surprised to find that Kirk Smith gave this 5-stars? I so often wish I had dropped everything I was doing and read all the books he suggested to me right away. I miss him.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8245664887619886757" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1288534840" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1288534840" />
    <a title="Doug H" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14987742-doug-h"><img alt="Doug H" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535994893p2/14987742.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1288534840?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Doug H" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Doug H" href="/user/show/14987742-doug-h">Doug H</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14987742-doug-h?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1288534840" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10873354555074532721"><br/>I loved everything about this novel, but I'd especially recommend it to people who prefer lyrical writing and character-driven storytelling over a thrill-filled plot. The story itself is good, but it takes the more scenic route home at times and sort of floats around in a way that makes it all feel a bit meditative. I actually loved it for that drowsy quality and read it slowly over several days.<br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/294731.Provinces_of_Night' title='Provinces of Night by William Gay' rel='nofollow noopener'>Provinces of Night</a> is simultaneously a coming-of-age story, a search for redemption and reparation, a</span>
  <span id="freeText10873354555074532721" style="display:none"><br>I loved everything about this novel, but I'd especially recommend it to people who prefer lyrical writing and character-driven storytelling over a thrill-filled plot. The story itself is good, but it takes the more scenic route home at times and sort of floats around in a way that makes it all feel a bit meditative. I actually loved it for that drowsy quality and read it slowly over several days.<br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/294731.Provinces_of_Night" title="Provinces of Night by William Gay" rel="nofollow noopener">Provinces of Night</a> is simultaneously a coming-of-age story, a search for redemption and reparation, a comedy, a tragedy, and a history of Fleming Bloodworth's extremely dysfunctional family. Gay's lush prose borders on poetry and his characters are so realistically developed that you find yourself suspecting that they were based on real people. Some of it is bright and laugh out loud funny - especially the episodes with Fleming's friend Junior Albright (who is not at all bright) and his Uncle Warren - but it's also dark and deeply tragic. What ties it all together is Gay's Gothic tone and painterly writing style. Masterpiece quality. <br><br><i>"The sky was absolutely cloudless and so blue it looked transparent and against it a wave of blackbirds shifted shapeless as smoke.”</i><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1445003143i/16586151._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13283923607115956575"><b>Life blindsides you so hard you can taste the bright copper blood in your mouth then it beguiles you with a gift of profound and appalling beauty.</b><br/><br/>Bloodworth is a name Dickens might have used for a protagonist, had he been born into Southern rural poverty in the wake of the Great Depression. It's a name that smartly summarizes the late William Gay's work; the taking and giving of life, the unshakable dividends of violent acts, and the ties that bind families across years and miles: the true worth</span>
  <span id="freeText13283923607115956575" style="display:none"><b>Life blindsides you so hard you can taste the bright copper blood in your mouth then it beguiles you with a gift of profound and appalling beauty.</b><br /><br />Bloodworth is a name Dickens might have used for a protagonist, had he been born into Southern rural poverty in the wake of the Great Depression. It's a name that smartly summarizes the late William Gay's work; the taking and giving of life, the unshakable dividends of violent acts, and the ties that bind families across years and miles: the true worth of one's own blood, could such a measure be taken. Alternatively oppressive and optimistic, depressing and uplifting, deadly serious and hilarious, and when you finish you take a deep contented breath, lingering over the final pages unable to let go of these people whose lives have become so important to you, and perhaps you flip back through the pages until you arrive again at the beginning and start over.</span>

<div id="review_1408359634" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1408359634" />
    <a title="Diane Barnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7262060-diane-barnes"><img alt="Diane Barnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1329773692p2/7262060.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1408359634?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane Barnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane Barnes" href="/user/show/7262060-diane-barnes">Diane Barnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7262060-diane-barnes?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1408359634" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7134520770378947475">"Life blindsides you so hard you can taste the bright copper blood on your mouth then it beguiles you with a gift of profound and appalling beauty". <br/><br/>What can be said about an author who writes lines like that? Not just one or two, but a whole novel? "A gift of profound and appalling beauty" is actually a perfect description of this book. I'll leave the synopsis to Goodreads and just tell you what I experienced while reading.<br/><br/>I was concurrently walking in the shoes of Fleming Bloodworth, a 17 year</span>
  <span id="freeText7134520770378947475" style="display:none">"Life blindsides you so hard you can taste the bright copper blood on your mouth then it beguiles you with a gift of profound and appalling beauty". <br /><br />What can be said about an author who writes lines like that? Not just one or two, but a whole novel? "A gift of profound and appalling beauty" is actually a perfect description of this book. I'll leave the synopsis to Goodreads and just tell you what I experienced while reading.<br /><br />I was concurrently walking in the shoes of Fleming Bloodworth, a 17 year old boy trying to make some sense out of life from reading books and observing people around him, and E.F.,his grandfather, trying to come to terms with a hard-lived life, bad decisions, and returning to his home to die. There are other members of this family, some of them crazy, drunk, violent, confused, but all of them trying to get by day to day in the best way they knew how. Junior Allbright was a family friend, Jack of all Trades, master of none character whose mess-ups provided a lot of humor. There was a lot of humor here in the very real dialogue between these people, and in their circumstances, even, maybe most especially, when things seemed hopeless. I shouldn't refer to them as characters, since they were very real to me, and I'm going to miss them, until I read this book again, which I most certainly will do.<br /><br />I have read "The Long Home" and "Twilight" and loved them both, but this is Gay's masterpiece. It is magnificent and it is beautiful.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7134520770378947475" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1138214676" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1138214676" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1138214676?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385499279" href="/book/show/953378.Provinces_of_Night">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=usa">usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible">audible</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=relationships">relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2015-read">2015-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1138214676" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14976456435862306096">Do you have a clear idea of what makes a Southern Gothic novel? I didn't before. I do now. Macabre tone, deeply flawed characters, a dash of voodoo, a derelict setting, sinister events, poverty, crime and violence. All this is delivered in the novel, but adjectives just don't draw the picture as well as Gay's story does. <br/><br/>The first half of the novel didn't deliver; the second half did. The first half is terribly slow. The momentum builds at the end. <br/><br/>The author often doesn't clarify who he is spea</span>
  <span id="freeText14976456435862306096" style="display:none">Do you have a clear idea of what makes a Southern Gothic novel? I didn't before. I do now. Macabre tone, deeply flawed characters, a dash of voodoo, a derelict setting, sinister events, poverty, crime and violence. All this is delivered in the novel, but adjectives just don't draw the picture as well as Gay's story does. <br /><br />The first half of the novel didn't deliver; the second half did. The first half is terribly slow. The momentum builds at the end. <br /><br />The author often doesn't clarify who he is speaking of. You think it is one person only to discover it is another. Very annoying, confusing and difficult to follow. By the books end you do end up understanding though. With the change of a paragraph the story can shift to a new person and place. You are given no warning and the characters are not necessarily specified; they are referred to as he or she! I found this annoying because the information I had just absorbed had been associated with the wrong individual. Rewinding and re-listening in an audiobook is a pain. <br /><br />Some of the lines are really good, and that is what kept me reading! <br /><br />Had it not been for the end I would never have given the book three stars! It is not a particularly neat ending but satisfying nevertheless. I understand the characters. I understand what happened. After struggling so hard this is in itself an achievement. The book is about a father, his three sons and two grandsons. Their women too. Messy relationships. Not all bad, but certainly not all good. Remember, this is a Southern Gothic novel!<br /><br />I hated the narration by Dick Hill, but as usual this does not affect my rating. When I listen to his narration I get the feeling that he thinks he is doing a stupendous dramatization. Others like theatrics; I don't. I had to think of the words and close out what I heard. His chuckles drove me batty.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14976456435862306096" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15419334100396218413">I wish I could be sitting on the porch listening to E.F. play his banjo right now. E.F. is a character not soon to be forgotten, similar to Larry Brown's Wade (see the books "Joe" and "Fay"). Both characters are old men doing scandalous acts - some funny and some not - but unforgettable nonetheless. <br/><br/><i>The Provinces of Night</i> is another excellent book by William Gay. He is soon becoming my favorite. This story encompasses so much that this review will not do it justice. I simply loved it. I loved th</span>
  <span id="freeText15419334100396218413" style="display:none">I wish I could be sitting on the porch listening to E.F. play his banjo right now. E.F. is a character not soon to be forgotten, similar to Larry Brown's Wade (see the books "Joe" and "Fay"). Both characters are old men doing scandalous acts - some funny and some not - but unforgettable nonetheless. <br /><br /><i>The Provinces of Night</i> is another excellent book by William Gay. He is soon becoming my favorite. This story encompasses so much that this review will not do it justice. I simply loved it. I loved the characters, the setting, and the language. Gay certainly had a talent with the written word. If I highlighted every sentence that struck me most of the book would be underlined. <br /><br />But what took me most by surprise was the humor which was not as profound in his previous book "The Long Home". There were several parts that had me in tears - literally. My favorite was the disastrous account of Fleming driving Warren and his "accountant". Downright hilarious. I was reading at work and couldn't stop laughing:<br /><br /><b>Now you're catchin on, Warren said. This flat black thing, I think that's what we're supposed to be drivin on. These woods and shit, I believe I'd just try to stay out of them as much as I could.</b><br /><br />If you're a fan of the Dirty South you need to read William Gay. <br /><br />Thank you Still for graciously sending me your extra copy. I cherished every word and will certainly keep to read again in the future.</span>

<div id="review_2543325200" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2543325200" />
    <a title="Wyndy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/74844479-wyndy"><img alt="Wyndy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581898027p2/74844479.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2543325200?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wyndy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wyndy" href="/user/show/74844479-wyndy">Wyndy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385499286" href="/book/show/1608946.Provinces_of_Night">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/74844479-wyndy?shelf=gothic-classic-and-southern">gothic-classic-and-southern</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/74844479-wyndy?shelf=2018-reads">2018-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/74844479-wyndy?shelf=favorites-keepers-to-reread">favorites-keepers-to-reread</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/74844479-wyndy?shelf=southern-us">southern-us</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/74844479-wyndy?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2543325200" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14831281071402669801">Poisonous snakes, angry hornets and cathead biscuits. I’ve said it before, and I’ll say it again - no true Southern Gothic novel is complete without them. But that’s where the similarity of this book to other gothic novels I’ve read ends. ‘Provinces Of Night’ is in a class of its own, maybe even a genre of its own - ‘Poetic Goth’ perhaps: “Came then plague days of desolation when loss ravaged him like a fever. The house was empty and dead without her. A place of ice, of perpetual winds.” ~ Flemi</span>
  <span id="freeText14831281071402669801" style="display:none">Poisonous snakes, angry hornets and cathead biscuits. I’ve said it before, and I’ll say it again - no true Southern Gothic novel is complete without them. But that’s where the similarity of this book to other gothic novels I’ve read ends. ‘Provinces Of Night’ is in a class of its own, maybe even a genre of its own - ‘Poetic Goth’ perhaps: “Came then plague days of desolation when loss ravaged him like a fever. The house was empty and dead without her. A place of ice, of perpetual winds.” ~ Fleming Bloodworth<br /><br />Wow.<br /><br />This is my first novel by William Gay, and the man was a maestro at creating believable backwoods Southern characters and realistic dialogue. I could picture these people - their dusty clothes, their weathered skin, their beat-up cars. I could hear their voices - “ort” for ought; “kindly” for kind of; “ever” for every. Set in the 1950’s in a corner of Tennessee, “70 miles back from Nashville,” this story leads us down the twisted path of the Bloodworth family where we meet patriarch E.F. Bloodworth; his sons Brady, Boyd and Warren; and his grandsons Fleming and Neal. Gay throws a stellar side character into this mix - the always fumbling but hilarious Junior Albright. Women are scattered throughout the story, but they are not the focus - they lie shimmering in the background trying to survive these men the best they can. Because these are men who follow their own path - for good or ill.<br /><br />One last requirement for a genuine Southern Gothic novel . . . the apocalyptic ending. And even in this, Gay writes pure poetry: “There was no life here. It was a world creating itself, caught in the caesura between the scraping away of the old order and the gestation of whatever altered form might follow.”<br /><br />For most of this novel, I had it pegged in my mind as a 4 because when I commit to a Southern Gothic novel, I prepare myself for a lot of grit. The first three quarters of this were a bit tame by genre standards, but immensely readable with beautiful prose. Then I hit “Book Four” where Gay forces every single character in this novel to make hard and gut wrenching decisions, life changing decisions. And the way Gay handled this section is nothing short of genius. This is a tour de force.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14831281071402669801" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_636192788" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/636192788" />
    <a title="Josh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10773168-josh"><img alt="Josh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1341723484p2/10773168.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/636192788?book_show_action=true">Jun 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josh" href="/user/show/10773168-josh">Josh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385499279" href="/book/show/953378.Provinces_of_Night">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10773168-josh?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer636192788" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17914932712396546864">Some link Gay to Cormac as the younger brother who can’t get out of the firstborn’s shadow. I am writing this review in part to debunk that concept; forgive me there will be a soapbox section at the tail-end. Before I get wound up though, the take away here is that this book touches all the bases to be great. It does more than touch each base; it slides hard into all of them....first and home included. It's a great character story. Fleming is pure hero. He's a high school dropout who is an acade</span>
  <span id="freeText17914932712396546864" style="display:none">Some link Gay to Cormac as the younger brother who can’t get out of the firstborn’s shadow. I am writing this review in part to debunk that concept; forgive me there will be a soapbox section at the tail-end. Before I get wound up though, the take away here is that this book touches all the bases to be great. It does more than touch each base; it slides hard into all of them....first and home included. It's a great character story. Fleming is pure hero. He's a high school dropout who is an academic by his own inert nature yet he hangs with and loves those who despise the finer points of living. EF is the deadbeat everyone loves, respects, and admires. Able to charm the uncharmable. A fighter, a lover, a magical being that tips his hat, tips his waitresses, and tips the scales in his own favor more often than not. Despite all these magnetisms, he’s the character you might call a "loser" if your real life ever crossed his path. I hate that admission because in my view this character may actually be autobiographic for Mr. Gay who I hate I'll now never have to opportunity to meet in real life. The book ends in a place that will leave the characters parked in your mind for a long time to come......wondering, hoping, believing (in that order). <br /><br />There are two opposite love stories each with their own twists, caveats, and conclusions. There are small side scenes that develop plot, setting, and character all at the same time. The snake in the path of cedars might be my favorite scene of all time. There was the perfect amount of musical and literary reference to other work that created intrigue but was not overdone to the point of making it seem forced. Unlike some books I esteem, there are tinges of victory over despair; fear not, desperation and ill-fated circumstance still abound. The rare and selfish feeling of not wanting to loan this text out after finishing despite my desire that others experience it triggers my wee small voice of guilt; give me a few weeks and I might let you read my copy. <br /><br />Now, for those trumpeting Gay only as a McCarthy mimicker....I would disagree. Certainly, he likely is an enthusiast like the rest of us (Heaven forbid one author be a "fanboy" of another). I can obviously see the links. The similarities between Bellwether (as just one example) and some of McCarthy's characters come to mind. I think he uses hawks caught in thermals very similarly. At times, I felt influences and overtones from The Orchard Keeper, Suttree, heck even Child of God was in there in places. Most of us who dwell around in this genre might take the same kinds of steps (not mistakes) if we set out to write our own tales. Gay's dive into these parallels was clearly conscious. Had he not "wanted" to draw those comparisons he was clearly gifted enough to avoid creating this discussion. Our life's flavor is seasoned with those we respect the most. For that matter, McCarthy (whom I love) took pieces of Faulkner and O'Conner and then developed his own unique voice. Gay represents his own voice in that same manner. He has an ability to say things that are profound and deep very simplistically. While he doesn’t require the same bedside dictionary as McCarthy, he writes with such master that almost no one could do something so elaborate but plain at the same time. It is akin to McCarthy without the distraction. Sometimes the distraction of McCarthy makes it hard to hear the alarm bells that need to be going off. McCarthy is better on second read than first. Most who would try to make McCarthyisms more digestible would not be able to evoke the same HUGE trains of thought. Gay accomplishes that in ways that we all SHOULD be able to do but can't. I love them both; two castoffs not looking for rescue on different islands (or better yet desolate, God forsaken stands of scrub pine) than point toward the same moon at the same dusky hour.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17914932712396546864" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText2102544741415084749" style="display:none">A lyrical and haunting tale about the Bloodworth family in early 1950's Tennessee.When the family patriarch EF wanders home after a twenty year absence, his 3 grown sons and his wife who has grown old and " feeble minded" are none to happy to see him. Fleming Bloodworth, I want to be in your gang.<br/><br/>Add everything said well, seductively, throw in voodoo, vengeance, well drawn characters, a gothic backdrop and you have Provinces of Night.   <span id="freeText15634794453115032177" style="display:none">Reading about a character who is as beguiled by books as you are in that moment, it's like a secret shared across time. Fleming Bloodworth, I want to be in your gang.<br /><br />Add everything said well, seductively, throw in voodoo, vengeance, well drawn characters, a gothic backdrop and you have Provinces of Night. I kind of overdosed on the first 20 pages and had to give it a rest. Gorgeous stuff.<br /><br />You also need time with it when the story rambles, as the characters become drowsy - you need to settle into it, but it's still magic.<br /><br />I disagree that this is a Cormac McCarthy rip off, William Gay tells his story using different tools, no better or worse but they both share the excellent skill of rendering the dark beautiful.</span>

  <span id="freeText16154525576780081199" style="display:none">I'm sorry, no plot summary in this review. You can read one of the other reviews or the book jacket. But I will tell you why you should read it. There is an aching poetic beauty, that never runs away with it self and becomes pretense. There is also straightforward simplicity that is never banal or ordinary. The characters with their burdens and hopes are vivid enough that you can smell them. The ending works, but you still walk away with things to chew on, you wonder about the characters once you close the book. This book is gorgeous.<br /><br />I was sorry when it ended. Then sad again the next day when I remembered I'd finished it. It left me lonely for more.</span>

<div id="review_2568540476" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2568540476" />
    <a title="Franky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2534471-franky"><img alt="Franky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1312356219p2/2534471.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2568540476?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Franky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Franky" href="/user/show/2534471-franky">Franky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385499279" href="/book/show/953378.Provinces_of_Night">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2534471-franky?shelf=southern">southern</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2534471-franky?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2534471-franky?shelf=southern-gothic">southern-gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2534471-franky?shelf=2018-book-challenge">2018-book-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2568540476" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12762714140034488121">This was my first experience reading of any of William Gay’s work and it did not disappoint. <br/><br/>Rather than following a traditional plot, Provinces of Night works as more of a series of episodes and scenes that all blend together to create the narrative. The novel centers in on the Bloodworth family in 1950s Tennessee. E.F. Bloodworth, the patriarch, has returned home after walking out on his wife and family twenty years prior. Much of the plot is devoted to focus on Bloodworth, his sons (Warren, B</span>
  <span id="freeText12762714140034488121" style="display:none">This was my first experience reading of any of William Gay’s work and it did not disappoint. <br /><br />Rather than following a traditional plot, Provinces of Night works as more of a series of episodes and scenes that all blend together to create the narrative. The novel centers in on the Bloodworth family in 1950s Tennessee. E.F. Bloodworth, the patriarch, has returned home after walking out on his wife and family twenty years prior. Much of the plot is devoted to focus on Bloodworth, his sons (Warren, Boyd, Brady) and his grandson (Fleming). <br /><br />I thought the characters were very well drawn. Many were very realistically portrayed, with a certain rawness, and unfiltered and gritty. I really felt invested and interested in the outcome of Fleming and enjoyed many of the scenes with him. The way that Fleming was developed definitely reminded me of a coming of age or bildungsroman in that there is a definite build up and progression of character from point A to point B as he learns about growing up. <br /><br />There are some influences from both William Falkner and Cormac McCarthy within Gay’s Provinces of Night. The book has a definite literary quality to it, not only in its depth and structure, but within its prose, with some beautifully constructed passages. Within the scope of the novel, there is much examination of the human condition and familial conflicts and bonds.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12762714140034488121" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2568540476">

<div id="review_1038787279" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1038787279" />
    <a title="Gea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3406281-gea"><img alt="Gea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473169564p2/3406281.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1038787279?book_show_action=true">Aug 28, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gea" href="/user/show/3406281-gea">Gea</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385499286" href="/book/show/1608946.Provinces_of_Night">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3406281-gea?shelf=2014-read">2014-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3406281-gea?shelf=southern-lit">southern-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3406281-gea?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3406281-gea?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1038787279" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13694274403331481042">William Gay was the son of a sharecropper from Tennessee with an ear for language and love of literature. A blue-collar worker with the soul of a poet, Gay read Thomas Wolfe, Cormac McCarthy and Flannery O'Connor when he wasn't painting houses or hanging drywall. Although he wrote for most of his life, his work was continually rejected by the literary scene. Finally, at 58 he published his first novel and the next year a bidding war ensued for Provinces of Night. He died at 70.<br/><br/><b>
  <blockquote>These hours bef</blockquote></b></span>
  <span id="freeText13694274403331481042" style="display:none">William Gay was the son of a sharecropper from Tennessee with an ear for language and love of literature. A blue-collar worker with the soul of a poet, Gay read Thomas Wolfe, Cormac McCarthy and Flannery O'Connor when he wasn't painting houses or hanging drywall. Although he wrote for most of his life, his work was continually rejected by the literary scene. Finally, at 58 he published his first novel and the next year a bidding war ensued for Provinces of Night. He died at 70.<br /><br /><b>
  <blockquote>These hours before first light were merciless. You could not go back to sleep and it was too early to get up and the things you had done or not done lay in your mind immovable as misshapen things you'd erected from stone. There was no give to these hours. They took no prisoners, made no compromises, and the things you had done could not be rationalized into anything save things you had done.</blockquote>
</b><br /><br />Provinces of Night is pure southern gothic, kindling with violence, beauty, and deep insight into the conflicting longings of human nature. It's about the ties of blood and their power to drown, the unforeseen karma of violence that flows far into the future, life (or death) affirming sex, and maybe, sometimes, love.<br /><br /> It's about the Bloodworth Family: the grand patriarch E.F., his sons Brady, Boyd and Warren; but it's mostly about 17-year-old Fleming, EF's grandson, abandoned by his parents to fend for himself with little more than a roof over his head and a wood burning stove. Fleming is on his own, but he has a toughness that only deep country can breed and a kindness the men of his family never had.<br /><br /><b>
  <blockquote>That's the craziest thing I ever heard in my life, his grandmother said. . . If sense was gunpowder, ever one of you men put together wouldn't have enough to load a round of birdshot.</blockquote>
</b><br /><br />The tenaciously self-destructive men and hard rural culture of Provinces reminded me of Daniel Woodrell's Winter's Bone. Fleming embodies a beautifully male version of Dee Rolley with grit born from hardship and an inner moral compass many around him don't seem to have. Life is unforgiving and its lessons hard, but Fleming never utters a single word of self-pity. It doesn't cross his mind.<br /><br /><b>
  <blockquote>Listen, he said vehemently. Somebody's going to have to say what they really mean and then do what they say they will. All this lying. All this bullshit and pretending. It's just wasting lives, wasting time, everything's just a waste.<br /><br />She looked at him curiously. That's just the way people are. The way the world is. What are you trying to do, fix the world?<br /><br />I don't want to fix the world. Fuck the world. Just the little part of it that I have to live on.</blockquote>
</b><br /><br />This is true literary fiction. It's not plot driven. It's about lives, souls, and what it means to be human. It's about how a young man with the odds stacked against him can possibly learn to survive the soul-shaking brutality and "appalling beauty of life."<br /><br /><b>
  <blockquote>He had no faith in the permanence of any of this. What he's seen of life had shown him that the world had little of comfort or assurance. He suspected that there were no givens, no map through the maze. Here in falling dark with the world rolling simultaneously toward him and away from him everything seemed no more than random. Life blindsides you so hard you can taste the bright copper blood in your mouth then it beguiles you with a gift of profound and appalling beauty.</blockquote>
</b><br /><br />William Gay knew a lot about life. While he was fixing houses and earning his daily bread, he was also watching, listening and taking everything in. I want to read everything he wrote, soak in his vision of the world, and hear its music.<br /><br /><br /><br /> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13694274403331481042" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2554158262" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2554158262" />
    <a title="Tina " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14260681-tina"><img alt="Tina " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1417033701p2/14260681.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2554158262?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tina " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tina " href="/user/show/14260681-tina">Tina </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14260681-tina?shelf=on-the-southern-literary-trail-li">on-the-southern-literary-trail-li</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14260681-tina?shelf=southern-books-to-read">southern-books-to-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2554158262" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10730744878278432200">It ended way before I was ready to give up the characters in this novel. I loved the short on spoken words, but big on deep thoughts character of Fleming Bloodworth from Ackerman’s Field, TN. Teenage Fleming with his brains and common sense amid a family of nut jobs, drunks, and criminals. Provinces of Night is laugh out loud funny at times and dark at others. Backwoods Middle,TN never looked more beautiful or menacing than in this novel. Lyrical sentences were Gay’s specialty and the characters</span>
  <span id="freeText10730744878278432200" style="display:none">It ended way before I was ready to give up the characters in this novel. I loved the short on spoken words, but big on deep thoughts character of Fleming Bloodworth from Ackerman’s Field, TN. Teenage Fleming with his brains and common sense amid a family of nut jobs, drunks, and criminals. Provinces of Night is laugh out loud funny at times and dark at others. Backwoods Middle,TN never looked more beautiful or menacing than in this novel. Lyrical sentences were Gay’s specialty and the characters of this novel unforgettable. <br /><br />Provinces of Night got William Gay recognized as the talented Southern writer that he was. Having read a bit recently about William Gay’s life, I think some of this novel was certainly autobiographical. William Gay was as much of a character as any of those he created in this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10730744878278432200" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2534683601" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2534683601" />
    <a title="James Aura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25078753-james-aura"><img alt="James Aura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443839874p2/25078753.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2534683601?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James Aura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James Aura" href="/user/show/25078753-james-aura">James Aura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2534683601" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11226329837791055550">Provinces of Night is a rough, deep redneck soap opera, very elegantly written. Gay had a way with words and an obvious knowledge of the southern rural poor. A dark story with a well-described cast of characters, most of whom seem to be doomed to the province named in the title. But one lad, apparently manages to escape. And that's all I'll say, except that it rings very true in my experience. <br/>A brilliant novel and I am sorry we have lost William Gay. </span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_2569158883" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roxy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roxy" href="/user/show/18417210-roxy">Roxy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2569158883" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8012430559075081758">This writer is just amazing at perfectly describing the country that these deeply flawed people inhabit. The cadence of their speech, the way they eat and live and love and hate, are thrust sharply into the reader’s mind, and cannot be dug out.</span>
