<span id="freeTextContainer13479817841587529557">William gay was a writer of profound lyrical skill, a master craftsman in storytelling holds a sheer beauty with words and language usage, arranges sentences in a way that makes reading his stories a joy. His words metaphors and similes paint wonderful scenes and thoughts of our surroundings and the human condition.<br/>This story is of discovery and redeeming through loss and love. The characters are memorable and from walks of life that liven the somber soul and broaden some narrow minds.<br/>His charac</span>
  <span id="freeText13479817841587529557" style="display:none">William gay was a writer of profound lyrical skill, a master craftsman in storytelling holds a sheer beauty with words and language usage, arranges sentences in a way that makes reading his stories a joy. His words metaphors and similes paint wonderful scenes and thoughts of our surroundings and the human condition.<br />This story is of discovery and redeeming through loss and love. The characters are memorable and from walks of life that liven the somber soul and broaden some narrow minds.<br />His characters in this tale live and breathe life to its edges and others modestly.<br />Kin, brothers and one man a father a grandfather to a budding young writer Fleming whose probably the only one that admires his grandpa the blues man, wanderer of the earth and of many adventures. Fleming is wanting to be far from his kin's past and make a difference he finds love in the form of another soul a female whose also had a shady upbringing this fact brings a little light through this story on his future and wills or wills not in settling down.<br />This was a wonderfully written human tragedy with originality and a language that some may not be accustomed that does nothing but add that extra hook to the reading experience.<br />By the time i finished i learned two things one word of expression <br />"Hellfire" and a few words "Shall i put a HEx(curse) on him" that will forever run in my quieter hours with a smile on my face.<br />An underrated writer not known enough by the masses who died in 2012.<br /><br />Excerpts to come by sunday 30th :)<br /><br />Check out some great videos with William Gay doing Readings and being interviewed on the review page here&gt;&gt;<a href="http://more2read.com/review/provinces-of-night-by-william-gay/" rel="nofollow noopener">http://more2read.com/review/provinces-of-night-by-william-gay/</a></span>

<div id="review_331331879" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/331331879" />
    <a title="Carl R." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9314075-carl-r"><img alt="Carl R." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1336319811p2/9314075.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/331331879?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carl R." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carl R." href="/user/show/9314075-carl-r">Carl R.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer331331879" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17331836899508155912">Let us now sing the praises of William Gay, which I have done twice before over the last months. But never enough. This occasion is Provinces of Night, the most challenging and profound of the now-trio of works that have fallen into my hands.<br/>We are as usual in rural Tennessee, town of Ackerman’s Field. Year 1952. Gay serves up a feast of complex characters and wonderful names. The Bloodworths—E.F., Fleming, Brady, Boyd. There’s itchy Mama, Sheriff Bellewether, Raven Lee Halfacre, Snowwhite Café,</span>
  <span id="freeText17331836899508155912" style="display:none">Let us now sing the praises of William Gay, which I have done twice before over the last months. But never enough. This occasion is Provinces of Night, the most challenging and profound of the now-trio of works that have fallen into my hands.<br />We are as usual in rural Tennessee, town of Ackerman’s Field. Year 1952. Gay serves up a feast of complex characters and wonderful names. The Bloodworths—E.F., Fleming, Brady, Boyd. There’s itchy Mama, Sheriff Bellewether, Raven Lee Halfacre, Snowwhite Café, Junior Albright.<br /><br />E.F. is a vagrant, wandering banjo picker who left his family years earlier, wants to come home to die of the stroke that has put him at death’s door. Fleming is the grandson who has known E.F. only by legend. Brady is the bitter, fortune-telling, hex-fixing son who stuck by his mother through the decades of the absent dad. Warren and Boyd are the disloyal sons who drift (not too far, though) from the homesite. Raven Lee is the 16-year-old beauty with the drunken, whorish mother whom Fleming worships and adores beyond his years and his capacity to control. Junior is Fleming’s ne’er do well best friend, who turns out to have an unexpected yearning for penance and redemption.<br /><br />Powerful literary figures from our world appear throughout, Thomas Wolfe dominating, nourishing the soil of what might otherwise be simple—if captivating—tales of the rural folk.<br /><br />One of the book’s first events concerns a magazine’s return of a handwritten manuscript that Fleming had dreamed would be the first step on his stairway to literary success, the first instance of a constant theme of creation and loss. E.F.’s banjo recordings and blues songs (soulful enough they’re mistaken for Negro records) are another. Then there is Raven Lee’s hunger for that blues music that so bespeaks her life.<br /><br />Behind it all is the story of a great flood, for the TVA is afoot, and the incipient dam will soon destroy what generations got, begot, built and buried.<br /><br />And then, of course, there’s Gay’s language. . . ..<br /><br /><br />The underwater road looked inexplicable, freighted with lost meaning, like some old imponderable road an ancient race had built to a place that no longer existed.<br /><br /><br />Echoes of Cormac McCarthy, worthy of William Faulkner. I mean it.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17331836899508155912" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a title="Kiwi Begs2Differ  ✎" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kiwi Begs2Differ  ✎" href="/user/show/12287031-kiwi-begs2differ">Kiwi Begs2Differ  ✎</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/8553245-provinces-of-night">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12287031-kiwi-begs2differ?shelf=gen-fiction-contemp">gen-fiction-contemp</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1427792066" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5460495961153138922">Southern lit. It starts slow and was confusing at first, I had difficulty in following the characters and connecting them to each other, but I was hooked on the beautiful descriptive language, as it puts a spell on you:<br/><i><br/>“The wind was at the trees like something alive and faint light quaked and died, flared and diminished far to the west and he held his breath waiting for the thunder. It finally came, so faint it was like a dream of thunder, a hoarse incoherent whisper, just a madman mumbling to h</i></span>
  <span id="freeText5460495961153138922" style="display:none">Southern lit. It starts slow and was confusing at first, I had difficulty in following the characters and connecting them to each other, but I was hooked on the beautiful descriptive language, as it puts a spell on you:<br /><i><br />“The wind was at the trees like something alive and faint light quaked and died, flared and diminished far to the west and he held his breath waiting for the thunder. It finally came, so faint it was like a dream of thunder, a hoarse incoherent whisper, just a madman mumbling to himself in the eaves of the world.”<br /></i><br />Gay's humor compensates the darkness of this gothic novel, the episode with the hog was hilarious! <br />I listened to the audio version by Dick Hill, which was great, his voice magically evokes the South for me.<br /></span>

    <a title="Kevin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11089210-kevin"><img alt="Kevin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1490515728p2/11089210.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/420607522?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kevin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kevin" href="/user/show/11089210-kevin">Kevin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11089210-kevin?shelf=near-perfect-books-that-i-adore">near-perfect-books-that-i-adore</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer420607522" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1935634361874329951">It's already a couple of months since I read this. Problem is, I'm so in awe of the quality of William Gay's writing, that I don't think I will ever be able to post a fitting review of this novel. Perhaps after the next time I read it, I might be able to review it properly, for I will surely be returning to the Bloodworths again and again. Oh, one thing I have noted, is that William Gay always nails those awkward dialogue exchanges between two people who have the hots for each other and are tryi</span>
  <span id="freeText1935634361874329951" style="display:none">It's already a couple of months since I read this. Problem is, I'm so in awe of the quality of William Gay's writing, that I don't think I will ever be able to post a fitting review of this novel. Perhaps after the next time I read it, I might be able to review it properly, for I will surely be returning to the Bloodworths again and again. Oh, one thing I have noted, is that William Gay always nails those awkward dialogue exchanges between two people who have the hots for each other and are trying too hard not to show it. And of course, his economic writing style is a joy from start to finish. For the moment anyway, he's my favourite author bar none.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1935634361874329951" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('/1G9oyBRAfqu8TNjYEATYdgkkfPPeQwPFW2n1m+4ADtBSeoOJ+jtqyrIve39WneKX4SCD+HURZSZbqBg85EhNA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

It tells the story of two young boxers out of Stockton, California: Ernie Munger and Billy Tully, one in his late teens, the other just turning thirty, whose seemingly parallel lives intersect for a time. Set in an ambiance of glittering dreams and drab realities, it tells of the two fighters\' struggles to escape the confinements of their existence, and of the men and women in their world. Fat City is a novel about the sporting life like no other ever written: without melodrama or false heroics, written with a truthfulness that is at once painful and beautiful.\n\nDenis Johnson: &quot;Between the ages of 19 and 25 I studied Leonard Gardner’s book so closely that I began to fear I’d never be able to write anything but imitations of it, so I swore it off(...)When I was about 34 (the same age Gardner was when he published his), my first novel came out. About a year later I borrowed Fat City from the library and read it. I could see immediately that ten years’ exile hadn’t saved me from the influence of its perfection — I’d taught myself to write in Gardner’s style, though not as well. And now, many years later, it’s still true: Leonard Gardner has something to say in every word I write.&quot;\n\nJoan Didion: &quot;Leonard Gardner\'s Fat City affected me more than any new fiction I have read in a long while, and I do not think it affected me only because I come from Fat City, or somewhere near it. He has got it exactly right--the hanging around gas stations, the field dust, the relentless oppressiveness of the weather, the bleak liaisons sealed on the levees and Greyhound buses--but he has done more than just get it down, he has made it a metaphor for the joyless in heart.&quot;\n\nDavid Wagoner: &quot;The people he writes about are alive and three-dimensional, and have that meaty, sweaty immediacy I admire in novels and find so seldom. It\'s an odd, interesting world he explores here--as tense and vivid as the prose.&quot;\n\nIvan Gold: &quot;Gardner writes with power, with an insider\'s knowledge, and with a vividness and love for his characters which redeem them even when they\'re lost and beaten.&quot;\n\nHarry Mark Petrakis: &quot;A man of real talent. He makes the savage world he writes of come alive to the point where the reader can smell the sweat, and feel the anguish of unremitting failure.&quot;\n\nRoss Macdonald: &quot;In his pity and art Gardner moves beyond race, beyond guilt and punishment, as Twain and Melville did, into a tragic forgiveness. I have seldom read a novel as beautiful and individual as this one.&quot;\n\n\nOriginally published in 1969, Fat City is an American classic whose stature has increased over the years. He falls under the spell of a &quot;blind&quot; street preacher named Asa Hawks and his degenerate fifteen-year-old daughter, Lily Sabbath.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/62659.William_Gay">About William Gay</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/62659.William_Gay"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1316108313p3/62659.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/62659.William_Gay">William Gay
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
353
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11681623491743925830">William Elbert Gay was the author of the novels <i>Provinces of Night</i>, <i>The Long Home</i>, and <i>Twilight</i> and the short story collection <i>I Hate to See That Evening Sun Go Down</i>. He was the winner of the 1999 William Peden Award and the 1999 James A. Michener Memorial Prize and the recipient of a 2002 Guggenheim Fellowship. </span>

</div>
</div>
